<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Detailed_process">Detailed process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Detailed process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Core_collapse_scenario.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="588" data-file-width="775" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Core_collapse_scenario.svg/310px-Core_collapse_scenario.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Core_collapse_scenario.svg/465px-Core_collapse_scenario.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Core_collapse_scenario.svg/620px-Core_collapse_scenario.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Core_collapse_scenario.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Within a massive, evolved star (a) the onion-layered shells of elements undergo fusion, forming an iron core (b) that reaches Chandrasekhar-mass and starts to collapse. The inner part of the core is compressed into neutrons (c), causing infalling material to bounce (d) and form an outward-propagating shock front (red). The shock starts to stall (e), but it is re-invigorated by a process that may include neutrino interaction. The surrounding material is blasted away (f), leaving only a degenerate remnant.</div></div></div>
<p>When a stellar core is no longer supported against gravity, it collapses in on itself with velocities reaching 70,000 km/s (0.23<a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light"><i>c</i></a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grav_waves_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-grav_waves-93">[93]</a></sup> resulting in a rapid increase in temperature and density. What follows next depends on the mass and structure of the collapsing core, with low-mass degenerate cores forming neutron stars, higher-mass degenerate cores mostly collapsing completely to black holes, and non-degenerate cores undergoing runaway fusion.
</p><p>The initial collapse of degenerate cores is accelerated by <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>, photodisintegration and electron capture, which causes a burst of <a href="/wiki/Electron_neutrino" title="Electron neutrino">electron neutrinos</a>. As the density increases, <a href="/wiki/Supernova_neutrinos" title="Supernova neutrinos">neutrino emission</a> is cut off as they become trapped in the core. The inner core eventually reaches typically 30 <a href="/wiki/Kilometre" title="Kilometre">km</a> in diameter<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WoosleyJanka_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-WoosleyJanka-94">[94]</a></sup> with a density comparable to that of an <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nucleus</a>, and neutron <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Degeneracy_pressure" title="Degeneracy pressure">degeneracy pressure</a> tries to halt the collapse. If the core mass is more than about 15 solar masses then neutron degeneracy is insufficient to stop the collapse and a black hole forms directly with no supernova.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renzon2018_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-renzon2018-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>In lower mass cores the collapse is stopped and the newly formed neutron core has an initial temperature of about 100 billion <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a>, 6,000 times the temperature of the sun's core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> At this temperature, neutrino-antineutrino pairs of all <a href="/wiki/Neutrino_oscillation" title="Neutrino oscillation">flavours</a> are efficiently formed by <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">thermal emission</a>. These thermal neutrinos are several times more abundant than the electron-capture neutrinos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> About 10<sup>46</sup> joules, approximately 10% of the star's rest mass, is converted into a ten-second burst of neutrinos, which is the main output of the event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WoosleyJanka_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-WoosleyJanka-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barwick_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-barwick-97">[97]</a></sup> The suddenly halted core collapse rebounds and produces a shock wave that stalls in the outer core within milliseconds<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> as energy is lost through the dissociation of heavy elements. A process that is not clearly understood<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> is necessary to allow the outer layers of the core to reabsorb around 10<sup>44</sup> joules<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barwick_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-barwick-97">[97]</a></sup> (1 <a href="/wiki/Foe_(unit)" title="Foe (unit)">foe</a>) from the neutrino pulse, producing the visible brightness, although there are also other theories on how to power the explosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WoosleyJanka_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-WoosleyJanka-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some material from the outer envelope falls back onto the neutron star, and, for cores beyond about 8 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub>, there is sufficient fallback to form a black hole. This fallback will reduce the kinetic energy created and the mass of expelled radioactive material, but in some situations, it may also generate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_jet" title="Relativistic jet">relativistic jets</a> that result in a gamma-ray burst or an exceptionally luminous supernova.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-piram2019_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-piram2019-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>The collapse of a massive non-degenerate core will ignite further fusion. When the core collapse is initiated by pair instability, oxygen fusion begins and the collapse may be halted. For core masses of 40–60 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub>, the collapse halts and the star remains intact, but collapse will occur again when a larger core has formed. For cores of around 60–130 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub>, the fusion of oxygen and heavier elements is so energetic that the entire star is disrupted, causing a supernova. At the upper end of the mass range, the supernova is unusually luminous and extremely long-lived due to many solar masses of ejected <sup>56</sup>Ni. For even larger core masses, the core temperature becomes high enough to allow photodisintegration and the core collapses completely into a black hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kasen_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-kasen-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-renzon2018_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-renzon2018-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Type_II_2">Type II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Type II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Type_II_supernova" title="Type II supernova">Type II supernova</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SN_1997D.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="463" data-file-width="399" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/SN_1997D.jpg/220px-SN_1997D.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/SN_1997D.jpg/330px-SN_1997D.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/74/SN_1997D.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SN_1997D.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The atypical subluminous type II <a href="/wiki/SN_1997D" title="SN 1997D">SN 1997D</a></div></div></div>
<p>Stars with initial masses less than about 8 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub> never develop a core large enough to collapse and they eventually lose their atmospheres to become white dwarfs. Stars with at least 9 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub> (possibly as much as 12 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-superagb_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-superagb-101">[101]</a></sup>) evolve in a complex fashion, progressively burning heavier elements at hotter temperatures in their cores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WoosleyJanka_94-3"><a href="#cite_note-WoosleyJanka-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science304_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-science304-102">[102]</a></sup> The star becomes layered like an onion, with the burning of more easily fused elements occurring in larger shells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heger_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-heger-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hinshaw_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-hinshaw-103">[103]</a></sup> Although popularly described as an onion with an iron core, the least massive supernova progenitors only have oxygen-<a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>(-<a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>) cores. These <a href="/wiki/Super-AGB_star" title="Super-AGB star">super-AGB stars</a> may form the majority of core collapse supernovae, although less luminous and so less commonly observed than those from more massive progenitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-superagb_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-superagb-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>If core collapse occurs during a supergiant phase when the star still has a hydrogen envelope, the result is a type II supernova.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horiuchi_et_al_2014_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horiuchi_et_al_2014-104">[104]</a></sup> The rate of mass loss for luminous stars depends on the metallicity and <a href="/wiki/Luminosity" title="Luminosity">luminosity</a>. Extremely luminous stars at near solar metallicity will lose all their hydrogen before they reach core collapse and so will not form a supernova of type II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horiuchi_et_al_2014_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-Horiuchi_et_al_2014-104">[104]</a></sup> At low metallicity, all stars will reach core collapse with a hydrogen envelope but sufficiently massive stars collapse directly to a black hole without producing a visible supernova.
</p><p>Stars with an initial mass up to about 90 times the sun, or a little less at high metallicity, result in a type II-P supernova, which is the most commonly observed type. At moderate to high metallicity, stars near the upper end of that mass range will have lost most of their hydrogen when core collapse occurs and the result will be a type II-L supernova.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> At very low metallicity, stars of around 140–250 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub> will reach core collapse by pair instability while they still have a hydrogen atmosphere and an oxygen core and the result will be a supernova with type II characteristics but a very large mass of ejected <sup>56</sup>Ni and high luminosity.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Type_Ib_and_Ic">Type Ib and Ic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Type Ib and Ic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Type_Ib_and_Ic_supernovae" title="Type Ib and Ic supernovae">Type Ib and Ic supernovae</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Supernova_2008D.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="538" data-file-width="1082" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Supernova_2008D.jpg/260px-Supernova_2008D.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Supernova_2008D.jpg/390px-Supernova_2008D.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Supernova_2008D.jpg/520px-Supernova_2008D.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Supernova_2008D.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>SN 2008D, a type Ib<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> supernova at the far upper end of the galaxy, shown in <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> (left) and visible light (right)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>These supernovae, like those of type II, are massive stars that undergo core collapse. However, the stars which become types Ib and Ic supernovae have lost most of their outer (hydrogen) envelopes due to strong <a href="/wiki/Stellar_wind" title="Stellar wind">stellar winds</a> or else from interaction with a companion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> These stars are known as Wolf–Rayet stars, and they occur at moderate to high metallicity where continuum driven winds cause sufficiently high mass-loss rates. Observations of type Ib/c supernova do not match the observed or expected occurrence of Wolf–Rayet stars. Alternate explanations for this type of core collapse supernova involve stars stripped of their hydrogen by binary interactions. Binary models provide a better match for the observed supernovae, with the proviso that no suitable binary helium stars have ever been observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eldridge_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-eldridge-109">[109]</a></sup> Since a supernova can occur whenever the mass of the star at the time of core collapse is low enough not to cause complete fallback to a black hole, any massive star may result in a supernova if it loses enough mass before core collapse occurs.
</p><p>Type Ib supernovae are the more common and result from Wolf–Rayet stars of type WC which still have helium in their atmospheres. For a narrow range of masses, stars evolve further before reaching core collapse to become WO stars with very little helium remaining, and these are the progenitors of type Ic supernovae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p><p>A few percent of the type Ic supernovae are associated with <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_burst" title="Gamma-ray burst">gamma-ray bursts</a> (GRB), though it is also believed that any hydrogen-stripped type Ib or Ic supernova could produce a GRB, depending on the circumstances of the geometry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The mechanism for producing this type of GRB is the jets produced by the magnetic field of the rapidly spinning <a href="/wiki/Magnetar" title="Magnetar">magnetar</a> formed at the collapsing core of the star. The jets would also transfer energy into the expanding outer shell, producing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Super-luminous_supernova" title="Super-luminous supernova">super-luminous supernova</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-piram2019_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-piram2019-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ultra-stripped supernovae occur when the exploding star has been stripped (almost) all the way to the metal core, via mass transfer in a close binary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TaurisLangerPodsiadlowski2015_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-TaurisLangerPodsiadlowski2015-115">[115]</a></sup>
As a result, very little material is ejected from the exploding star (c. 0.1 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub>). In the most extreme cases, ultra-stripped supernovae can occur in naked metal cores, barely above the Chandrasekhar mass limit. SN 2005ek<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> might be the first observational example of an ultra-stripped supernova, giving rise to a relatively dim and fast decaying light curve. The nature of ultra-stripped supernovae can be both iron core-collapse and electron capture supernovae, depending on the mass of the collapsing core. Ultra-stripped supernovae are believed to be associated with the second supernova explosion in a binary system, e.g. producing a tight double neutron star system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TaurisKramerFreire2017_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-TaurisKramerFreire2017-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeKasliwalOfek2018_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeKasliwalOfek2018-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2022 a team of astronomers led by researchers from the Weizmann Institute of Science reported the first supernova explosion showing direct evidence for a Wolf-Rayet progenitor star. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SN_2019hgp&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SN 2019hgp (page does not exist)">SN 2019hgp</a> was a type Icn supernova and is also the first in which the element neon has been detected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Electron-capture_supernovae"><span class="anchor" id="Electron-capture_supernovae"></span> Electron-capture supernovae</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Electron-capture supernovae">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1980, a "third type" of supernova was predicted by Ken'ichi Nomoto of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Tokyo" title="University of Tokyo">University of Tokyo</a>, called an electron-capture supernova. It would arise when a star "in the transitional range (~8 to 10 solar masses) between white dwarf formation and iron core-collapse supernovae", and with a <a href="/wiki/Degenerate_matter" title="Degenerate matter">degenerate</a> O+Ne+Mg core,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HowellEtAl2021-01a_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-HowellEtAl2021-01a-121">[121]</a></sup> imploded after its core ran out of nuclear fuel, causing gravity to compress the <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> in the star's core into their atomic nuclei,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KeckObsNews2021-06-28-01a_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-KeckObsNews2021-06-28-01a-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RTE2021-06-28-01a_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-RTE2021-06-28-01a-123">[123]</a></sup> leading to a supernova explosion and leaving behind a neutron star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heger_89-3"><a href="#cite_note-heger-89">[89]</a></sup> In June 2021, a paper in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_Astronomy" title="Nature Astronomy">Nature Astronomy</a></i> reported that the 2018 supernova <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SN_2018zd" title="SN 2018zd">SN 2018zd</a> (in the galaxy <a href="/wiki/NGC_2146" title="NGC 2146">NGC 2146</a>, about 31 million light years from Earth) appeared to be the first observation of an electron-capture supernova.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HowellEtAl2021-01a_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-HowellEtAl2021-01a-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KeckObsNews2021-06-28-01a_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-KeckObsNews2021-06-28-01a-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RTE2021-06-28-01a_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-RTE2021-06-28-01a-123">[123]</a></sup> The 1054 supernova explosion that created the Crab Nebula in our galaxy had been thought to be the best candidate for an electron-capture supernova, and the 2021 paper makes it more likely that this was correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KeckObsNews2021-06-28-01a_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-KeckObsNews2021-06-28-01a-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RTE2021-06-28-01a_123-2"><a href="#cite_note-RTE2021-06-28-01a-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Failed_supernovae">Failed supernovae</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Failed supernovae">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Failed_supernova" title="Failed supernova">Failed supernova</a></div>
<p>The core collapse of some massive stars may not result in a visible supernova. This happens if the initial core collapse cannot be reversed by the mechanism that produces an explosion, usually because the core is too massive. These events are difficult to detect, but large surveys have detected possible candidates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-failed_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-failed-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gerke_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-gerke-125">[125]</a></sup> The red supergiant <a href="/wiki/N6946-BH1" title="N6946-BH1">N6946-BH1</a> in <a href="/wiki/NGC_6946" title="NGC 6946">NGC 6946</a> underwent a modest outburst in March 2009, before fading from view. Only a faint <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> source remains at the star's location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adams_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-adams-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Light_curves">Light curves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Light curves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Comparative_supernova_type_light_curves.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1187" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Comparative_supernova_type_light_curves.png/330px-Comparative_supernova_type_light_curves.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Comparative_supernova_type_light_curves.png/495px-Comparative_supernova_type_light_curves.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Comparative_supernova_type_light_curves.png/660px-Comparative_supernova_type_light_curves.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Comparative_supernova_type_light_curves.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparative supernova type light curves</div></div></div>
<p>A historic puzzle concerned the source of energy that can maintain the optical supernova glow for months. The ejecta gases would dim quickly without some energy input to keep it hot. Some have considered rotational energy from the central pulsar as a source. Although the energy that initially powers each type of supernovae is delivered promptly, the light curves are dominated by subsequent radioactive heating of the rapidly expanding ejecta. The intensely radioactive nature of the ejecta gases was first calculated on sound nucleosynthesis grounds in the late 1960s, and this has since been demonstrated as correct for most supernovae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> It was not until <a href="/wiki/SN_1987A" title="SN 1987A">SN 1987A</a> that direct observation of gamma-ray lines unambiguously identified the major radioactive nuclei.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S.M._Matz,_G.H._Share_1988_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-S.M._Matz,_G.H._Share_1988-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is now known by direct observation that much of the light curve (the graph of luminosity as a function of time) after the occurrence of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_II_Supernova" title="Type II Supernova">type II Supernova</a>, such as SN 1987A, is explained by those predicted radioactive decays. Although the luminous emission consists of optical photons, it is the radioactive power absorbed by the ejected gases that keeps the remnant hot enough to radiate light. The radioactive decay of <sup>56</sup>Ni through its daughters <sup>56</sup>Co to <sup>56</sup>Fe produces gamma-ray <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a>, primarily with energies of <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6987135704348448899♠"></span>847 <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">keV</a></span> and <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6987198349449090600♠"></span>1,238 keV</span>, that are absorbed and dominate the heating and thus the luminosity of the ejecta at intermediate times (several weeks) to late times (several months).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Energy for the peak of the light curve of SN1987A was provided by the decay of <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_nickel" title="Isotopes of nickel"><sup>56</sup>Ni</a> to <sup>56</sup>Co (half-life 6 days) while energy for the later light curve in particular fit very closely with the 77.3-day half-life of <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_cobalt" title="Isotopes of cobalt"><sup>56</sup>Co</a> decaying to <sup>56</sup>Fe. Later measurements by space gamma-ray telescopes of the small fraction of the <sup>56</sup>Co and <sup>57</sup>Co gamma rays that escaped the SN 1987A remnant without absorption confirmed earlier predictions that those two radioactive nuclei were the power sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-S.M._Matz,_G.H._Share_1988_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-S.M._Matz,_G.H._Share_1988-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Messier_61_with_SN2020jfo_(Supernova).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1623" data-file-width="1845" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Messier_61_with_SN2020jfo_%28Supernova%29.jpg/220px-Messier_61_with_SN2020jfo_%28Supernova%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Messier_61_with_SN2020jfo_%28Supernova%29.jpg/330px-Messier_61_with_SN2020jfo_%28Supernova%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Messier_61_with_SN2020jfo_%28Supernova%29.jpg/440px-Messier_61_with_SN2020jfo_%28Supernova%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Messier_61_with_SN2020jfo_(Supernova).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Messier_61" title="Messier 61">Messier 61</a> with supernova SN2020jfo, taken by an amateur astronomer in 2020</div></div></div>
<p>The visual light curves of the different supernova types all depend at late times on radioactive heating, but they vary in shape and amplitude because of the underlying mechanisms, the way that visible radiation is produced, the epoch of its observation, and the transparency of the ejected material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> The light curves can be significantly different at other wavelengths. For example, at ultraviolet wavelengths there is an early extremely luminous peak lasting only a few hours corresponding to the breakout of the shock launched by the initial event, but that breakout is hardly detectable optically.
</p><p>The light curves for type Ia are mostly very uniform, with a consistent maximum absolute magnitude and a relatively steep decline in luminosity. Their optical energy output is driven by radioactive decay of ejected nickel-56 (half-life 6 days), which then decays to radioactive cobalt-56 (half-life 77 days). These radioisotopes excite the surrounding material to incandescence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-explosion_model_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-explosion_model-73">[73]</a></sup> Studies of cosmology today rely on <sup>56</sup>Ni radioactivity providing the energy for the optical brightness of supernovae of type Ia, which are the "standard candles" of cosmology but whose diagnostic <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6987135704348448899♠"></span>847 keV</span> and <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="6987198349449090600♠"></span>1,238 keV</span> gamma rays were first detected only in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> The initial phases of the light curve decline steeply as the effective size of the <a href="/wiki/Photosphere" title="Photosphere">photosphere</a> decreases and trapped electromagnetic radiation is depleted. The light curve continues to decline in the <a href="/wiki/UBV_photometric_system" title="UBV photometric system">B band</a> while it may show a small shoulder in the visual at about 40 days, but this is only a hint of a secondary maximum that occurs in the infra-red as certain ionised heavy elements recombine to produce infra-red radiation and the ejecta become transparent to it. The visual light curve continues to decline at a rate slightly greater than the decay rate of the radioactive cobalt (which has the longer half-life and controls the later curve), because the ejected material becomes more diffuse and less able to convert the high energy radiation into visual radiation. After several months, the light curve changes its decline rate again as <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission" title="Positron emission">positron emission</a> becomes dominant from the remaining cobalt-56, although this portion of the light curve has been little-studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taubenberger2009_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-taubenberger2009-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>Type Ib and Ic light curves are basically similar to type Ia although with a lower average peak luminosity. The visual light output is again due to radioactive decay being converted into visual radiation, but there is a much lower mass of the created nickel-56. The peak luminosity varies considerably and there are even occasional type Ib/c supernovae orders of magnitude more and less luminous than the norm. The most luminous type Ic supernovae are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Hypernova" title="Hypernova">hypernovae</a> and tend to have broadened light curves in addition to the increased peak luminosity. The source of the extra energy is thought to be relativistic jets driven by the formation of a rotating black hole, which also produce gamma-ray bursts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arxiv_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-arxiv-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>The light curves for type II supernovae are characterised by a much slower decline than type I, on the order of 0.05 magnitudes per day,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> excluding the plateau phase. The visual light output is dominated by kinetic energy rather than radioactive decay for several months, due primarily to the existence of hydrogen in the ejecta from the atmosphere of the supergiant progenitor star. In the initial destruction this hydrogen becomes heated and ionised. The majority of type II supernovae show a prolonged plateau in their light curves as this hydrogen recombines, emitting visible light and becoming more transparent. This is then followed by a declining light curve driven by radioactive decay although slower than in type I supernovae, due to the efficiency of conversion into light by all the hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doggett_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-doggett-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>In type II-L the plateau is absent because the progenitor had relatively little hydrogen left in its atmosphere, sufficient to appear in the spectrum but insufficient to produce a noticeable plateau in the light output. In type IIb supernovae the hydrogen atmosphere of the progenitor is so depleted (thought to be due to tidal stripping by a companion star) that the light curve is closer to a type I supernova and the hydrogen even disappears from the spectrum after several weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doggett_55-3"><a href="#cite_note-doggett-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Type IIn supernovae are characterised by additional narrow spectral lines produced in a dense shell of circumstellar material. Their light curves are generally very broad and extended, occasionally also extremely luminous and referred to as a superluminous supernova. These light curves are produced by the highly efficient conversion of kinetic energy of the ejecta into electromagnetic radiation by interaction with the dense shell of material. This only occurs when the material is sufficiently dense and compact, indicating that it has been produced by the progenitor star itself only shortly before the supernova occurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fillipenko1997_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-fillipenko1997-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pastorello2002_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-pastorello2002-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>Large numbers of supernovae have been catalogued and classified to provide distance candles and test models. Average characteristics vary somewhat with distance and type of host galaxy, but can broadly be specified for each supernova type.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asymmetry">Asymmetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Asymmetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chandra-crab.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="2400" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Chandra-crab.jpg/220px-Chandra-crab.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Chandra-crab.jpg/330px-Chandra-crab.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Chandra-crab.jpg/440px-Chandra-crab.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chandra-crab.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Pulsar" title="Pulsar">pulsar</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Crab_Nebula" title="Crab Nebula">Crab Nebula</a> is travelling at 375 km/s relative to the nebula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A long-standing puzzle surrounding type II supernovae is why the remaining compact object receives a large velocity away from the epicentre;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pulsar" title="Pulsar">pulsars</a>, and thus neutron stars, are observed to have high <a href="/wiki/Peculiar_velocity" title="Peculiar velocity">peculiar velocities</a>, and black holes presumably do as well, although they are far harder to observe in isolation. The initial impetus can be substantial, propelling an object of more than a solar mass at a velocity of 500 km/s or greater. This indicates an expansion asymmetry, but the mechanism by which momentum is transferred to the compact object remains<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> a puzzle. Proposed explanations for this kick include convection in the collapsing star and jet production during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutron_star_formation" title="Neutron star formation">neutron star formation</a>.
</p><p>One possible explanation for this asymmetry is large-scale <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a> above the core. The convection can create variations in the local abundances of elements, resulting in uneven nuclear burning during the collapse, bounce and resulting expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another possible explanation is that accretion of gas onto the central neutron star can create a <a href="/wiki/Accretion_disk" title="Accretion disk">disk</a> that drives highly directional jets, propelling matter at a high velocity out of the star, and driving transverse shocks that completely disrupt the star. These jets might play a crucial role in the resulting supernova.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> (A similar model is now favored for explaining long gamma-ray bursts.)
</p><p>Initial asymmetries have also been confirmed in type Ia supernovae through observation. This result may mean that the initial luminosity of this type of supernova depends on the viewing angle. However, the expansion becomes more symmetrical with the passage of time. Early asymmetries are detectable by measuring the polarization of the emitted light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_output">Energy output</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Energy output">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SNIacurva.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="214" data-file-width="299" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/SNIacurva.png/220px-SNIacurva.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/88/SNIacurva.png 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SNIacurva.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The radioactive decays of nickel-56 and cobalt-56 that produce a supernova visible light curve</div></div></div>
<p>Although supernovae are primarily known as luminous events, the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a> they release is almost a minor side-effect. Particularly in the case of core collapse supernovae, the emitted electromagnetic radiation is a tiny fraction of the total energy released during the event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a fundamental difference between the balance of energy production in the different types of supernova. In type Ia white dwarf detonations, most of the energy is directed into <a href="/wiki/Nucleosynthesis" title="Nucleosynthesis">heavy element synthesis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> of the ejecta.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> In core collapse supernovae, the vast majority of the energy is directed into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supernova_neutrino" title="Supernova neutrino">neutrino</a> emission, and while some of this apparently powers the observed destruction, 99%+ of the neutrinos escape the star in the first few minutes following the start of the collapse.
</p><p>Standard Type Ia supernovae derive their energy from a runaway nuclear fusion of a carbon-oxygen white dwarf. The details of the energetics are still not fully understood, but the end result is the ejection of the entire mass of the original star at high kinetic energy. Around half a solar mass of that mass is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nickel-56" title="Nickel-56"><sup>56</sup>Ni</a> generated from <a href="/wiki/Silicon-burning_process" title="Silicon-burning process">silicon burning</a>. <sup>56</sup>Ni is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a> and decays into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cobalt-56" title="Cobalt-56"><sup>56</sup>Co</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta_plus_decay" title="Beta plus decay">beta plus decay</a> (with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Half_life" title="Half life">half life</a> of six days) and gamma rays. <sup>56</sup>Co itself decays by the beta plus (<a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a>) path with a half life of 77 days into stable <sup>56</sup>Fe. These two processes are responsible for the electromagnetic radiation from type Ia supernovae. In combination with the changing transparency of the ejected material, they produce the rapidly declining light curve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mazzali_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-mazzali-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Core collapse supernovae are on average visually fainter than type Ia supernovae, but the total energy released is far higher. In these type of supernovae, the gravitational potential energy is converted into kinetic energy that compresses and collapses the core, initially producing electron neutrinos from disintegrating nucleons, followed by all <a href="/wiki/Flavour_(particle_physics)" title="Flavour (particle physics)">flavours</a> of thermal neutrinos from the super-heated neutron star core. Around 1% of these neutrinos are thought to deposit sufficient energy into the outer layers of the star to drive the resulting catastrophe, but again the details cannot be reproduced exactly in current models. Kinetic energies and nickel yields are somewhat lower than type Ia supernovae, hence the lower peak visual luminosity of type II supernovae, but energy from the de-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionisation" title="Ionisation">ionisation</a> of the many solar masses of remaining hydrogen can contribute to a much slower decline in luminosity and produce the plateau phase seen in the majority of core collapse supernovae.
</p>

<p>In some core collapse supernovae, fallback onto a black hole drives <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_jet" title="Relativistic jet">relativistic jets</a> which may produce a brief energetic and directional burst of gamma rays and also transfers substantial further energy into the ejected material. This is one scenario for producing high-luminosity supernovae and is thought to be the cause of type Ic hypernovae and long-duration gamma-ray bursts. If the relativistic jets are too brief and fail to penetrate the stellar envelope then a low luminosity gamma-ray burst may be produced and the supernova may be sub-luminous.
</p><p>When a supernova occurs inside a small dense cloud of circumstellar material, it will produce a shock wave that can efficiently convert a high fraction of the kinetic energy into electromagnetic radiation. Even though the initial energy was entirely normal the resulting supernova will have high luminosity and extended duration since it does not rely on exponential radioactive decay. This type of event may cause type IIn hypernovae.
</p><p>Although pair-instability supernovae are core collapse supernovae with spectra and light curves similar to type II-P, the nature after core collapse is more like that of a giant type Ia with runaway fusion of carbon, oxygen, and silicon. The total energy released by the highest-mass events is comparable to other core collapse supernovae but neutrino production is thought to be very low, hence the kinetic and electromagnetic energy released is very high. The cores of these stars are much larger than any white dwarf and the amount of radioactive nickel and other heavy elements ejected from their cores can be orders of magnitude higher, with consequently high visual luminosity.
</p>

Each exploding star briefly rivals the brightness of its host galaxy.</div></div></div>
<p>The supernova classification type is closely tied to the type of star at the time of the collapse. The occurrence of each type of supernova depends dramatically on the metallicity, and hence the age of the host galaxy.
</p><p>Type Ia supernovae are produced from white dwarf stars in binary star systems and occur in all <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_morphological_classification" title="Galaxy morphological classification">galaxy types</a>. Core collapse supernovae are only found in galaxies undergoing current or very recent star formation, since they result from short-lived massive stars. They are most commonly found in type Sc spirals, but also in the arms of other spiral galaxies and in <a href="/wiki/Irregular_galaxy" title="Irregular galaxy">irregular galaxies</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Starburst_galaxy" title="Starburst galaxy">starburst galaxies</a>.
</p><p>Type Ib/c and II-L, and possibly most type IIn, supernovae are only thought to be produced from stars having near-solar metallicity levels that result in high mass loss from massive stars, hence they are less common in older, more-distant galaxies. The table shows the progenitor for the main types of core collapse supernova, and the approximate proportions that have been observed in the local neighbourhood.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Fraction of core collapse supernovae types by progenitor<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eldridge_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-eldridge-109">[109]</a></sup>

<tbody><tr>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>There are a number of difficulties reconciling modelled and observed stellar evolution leading up to core collapse supernovae. Red supergiants are the progenitors for the vast majority of core collapse supernovae, and these have been observed but only at relatively low masses and luminosities, below about 18 <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub> and 100,000 <var>L</var><sub>☉</sub>, respectively. Most progenitors of type II supernovae are not detected and must be considerably fainter, and presumably less massive. This discrepancy has been referred to as the <b>red supergiant problem</b>. It was first described in 2009 by Stephen Smartt, who also coined the term. After performing a volume-limited search for supernovae, Smartt et al. found the lower and upper mass limits for type II-P supernovae to form to be <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7000850000000000000♠"></span>8.5<span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right;">+1<br/>−1.5</span></span></span> <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub> and <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7001165000000000000♠"></span>16.5<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">±</span>1.5</span> <var>M</var><sub>☉</sub> respectively. The former is consistent with the expected upper mass limits for white dwarf progenitors to form, but the latter is not consistent with massive star populations in the Local Group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smartt2009_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-smartt2009-153">[153]</a></sup> The upper limit for red supergiants that produce a visible supernova explosion has been calculated at <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7031377824500000000♠"></span>19<span style="margin-left:0.3em;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:right;">+4<br/>−2</span></span> <i>M</i><sub>☉</sub></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-davies2020_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-davies2020-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is now proposed that higher mass red supergiants do not explode as supernovae, but instead evolve back towards hotter temperatures. Several progenitors of type IIb supernovae have been confirmed, and these were K and G supergiants, plus one A supergiant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smartt_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-smartt-155">[155]</a></sup> Yellow hypergiants or LBVs are proposed progenitors for type IIb supernovae, and almost all type IIb supernovae near enough to observe have shown such progenitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-problem_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-problem-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ysg_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-ysg-157">[157]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:An_isolated_neutron_star_in_the_Small_Magellanic_Cloud.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1509" data-file-width="1510" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/An_isolated_neutron_star_in_the_Small_Magellanic_Cloud.jpg/220px-An_isolated_neutron_star_in_the_Small_Magellanic_Cloud.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/An_isolated_neutron_star_in_the_Small_Magellanic_Cloud.jpg/330px-An_isolated_neutron_star_in_the_Small_Magellanic_Cloud.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/An_isolated_neutron_star_in_the_Small_Magellanic_Cloud.jpg/440px-An_isolated_neutron_star_in_the_Small_Magellanic_Cloud.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:An_isolated_neutron_star_in_the_Small_Magellanic_Cloud.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Isolated neutron star in the <a href="/wiki/Small_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Small Magellanic Cloud">Small Magellanic Cloud</a></div></div></div>
<p>Until just a few decades ago, hot supergiants were not considered likely to explode, but observations have shown otherwise. Blue supergiants form an unexpectedly high proportion of confirmed supernova progenitors, partly due to their high luminosity and easy detection, while not a single Wolf–Rayet progenitor has yet been clearly identified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smartt_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-smartt-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Models have had difficulty showing how blue supergiants lose enough mass to reach supernova without progressing to a different evolutionary stage. One study has shown a possible route for low-luminosity post-red supergiant luminous blue variables to collapse, most likely as a type IIn supernova.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lbv_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-lbv-159">[159]</a></sup> Several examples of hot luminous progenitors of type IIn supernovae have been detected: <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SN_2005gy&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SN 2005gy (page does not exist)">SN 2005gy</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=SN_2010jl&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="SN 2010jl (page does not exist)">SN 2010jl</a> were both apparently massive luminous stars, but are very distant; and <a href="/wiki/SN_2009ip" title="SN 2009ip">SN 2009ip</a> had a highly luminous progenitor likely to have been an LBV, but is a peculiar supernova whose exact nature is disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smartt_155-2"><a href="#cite_note-smartt-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>The progenitors of type Ib/c supernovae are not observed at all, and constraints on their possible luminosity are often lower than those of known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WC_star" title="WC star">WC stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smartt_155-3"><a href="#cite_note-smartt-155">[155]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WO_star" title="WO star">WO stars</a> are extremely rare and visually relatively faint, so it is difficult to say whether such progenitors are missing or just yet to be observed. Very luminous progenitors have not been securely identified, despite numerous supernovae being observed near enough that such progenitors would have been clearly imaged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yoon_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-yoon-160">[160]</a></sup> Population modelling shows that the observed type Ib/c supernovae could be reproduced by a mixture of single massive stars and stripped-envelope stars from interacting binary systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eldridge_109-2"><a href="#cite_note-eldridge-109">[109]</a></sup> The continued lack of unambiguous detection of progenitors for normal type Ib and Ic supernovae may be due to most massive stars collapsing directly to a black hole <a href="/wiki/Failed_supernova" title="Failed supernova">without a supernova outburst</a>. Most of these supernovae are then produced from lower-mass low-luminosity helium stars in binary systems. A small number would be from rapidly-rotating massive stars, likely corresponding to the highly-energetic type Ic-BL events that are associated with long-duration gamma-ray bursts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smartt_155-4"><a href="#cite_note-smartt-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="External_impact">External impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: External impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Supernovae events generate heavier elements that are scattered throughout the surrounding interstellar medium. The expanding shock wave from a supernovae can trigger star formation. Galactic cosmic rays are generated by supernova explosions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Source_of_heavy_elements">Source of heavy elements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Source of heavy elements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Stellar_nucleosynthesis" title="Stellar nucleosynthesis">Stellar nucleosynthesis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Supernova_nucleosynthesis" title="Supernova nucleosynthesis">Supernova nucleosynthesis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nucleosynthesis_periodic_table.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Nucleosynthesis_periodic_table.svg/260px-Nucleosynthesis_periodic_table.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Nucleosynthesis_periodic_table.svg/390px-Nucleosynthesis_periodic_table.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Nucleosynthesis_periodic_table.svg/520px-Nucleosynthesis_periodic_table.svg.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nucleosynthesis_periodic_table.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Periodic table showing the source of each element in the interstellar medium</div></div></div>
<p>Supernovae are a major source of elements in the interstellar medium from oxygen through to rubidium,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nucleosynthesis_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-nucleosynthesis-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-François_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-François-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Truran_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-Truran-163">[163]</a></sup> though the theoretical abundances of the elements produced or seen in the spectra varies significantly depending on the various supernova types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Truran_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-Truran-163">[163]</a></sup> Type Ia supernovae produce mainly silicon and iron-peak elements, metals such as nickel and iron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nomoto2018_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-nomoto2018-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maeda_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-maeda-165">[165]</a></sup> Core collapse supernovae eject much smaller quantities of the iron-peak elements than type Ia supernovae, but larger masses of light <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha_element" title="Alpha element">alpha elements</a> such as oxygen and neon, and elements heavier than zinc. The latter is especially true with electron capture supernovae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> The bulk of the material ejected by type II supernovae is hydrogen and helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eichler_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-eichler-167">[167]</a></sup> The heavy elements are produced by: nuclear fusion for nuclei up to <sup>34</sup>S; silicon photodisintegration rearrangement and quasiequilibrium during silicon burning for nuclei between <sup>36</sup>Ar and <sup>56</sup>Ni; and rapid capture of neutrons (<a href="/wiki/R-process" title="R-process">r-process</a>) during the supernova's collapse for elements heavier than iron.  The r-process produces highly unstable nuclei that are rich in <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> and that rapidly beta decay into more stable forms. In supernovae, r-process reactions are responsible for about half of all the isotopes of elements beyond iron,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> although <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star_merger" title="Neutron star merger">neutron star mergers</a> may be the main astrophysical source for many of these elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nucleosynthesis_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-nucleosynthesis-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-siegel_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-siegel-169">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the modern universe, old <a href="/wiki/Asymptotic_giant_branch" title="Asymptotic giant branch">asymptotic giant branch</a> (AGB) stars are the dominant source of dust from <a href="/wiki/S-process" title="S-process">s-process</a> elements, oxides, and carbon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nucleosynthesis_161-2"><a href="#cite_note-nucleosynthesis-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> However, in the early universe, before AGB stars formed, supernovae may have been the main source of dust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dust_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-dust-171">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_in_stellar_evolution">Role in stellar evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Role in stellar evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Supernova_remnant" title="Supernova remnant">Supernova remnant</a></div>
<p>Remnants of many supernovae consist of a compact object and a rapidly expanding shock wave of material. This cloud of material sweeps up surrounding <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a> during a free expansion phase, which can last for up to two centuries. The wave then gradually undergoes a period of <a href="/wiki/Adiabatic_process" title="Adiabatic process">adiabatic expansion</a>, and will slowly cool and mix with the surrounding interstellar medium over a period of about 10,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:STScl-2005-15.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="658" data-file-width="867" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/STScl-2005-15.png/220px-STScl-2005-15.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/STScl-2005-15.png/330px-STScl-2005-15.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/STScl-2005-15.png/440px-STScl-2005-15.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:STScl-2005-15.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Supernova remnant N 63A lies within a clumpy region of gas and dust in the <a href="/wiki/Large_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Large Magellanic Cloud">Large Magellanic Cloud</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> produced hydrogen, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, and traces of <a href="/wiki/Lithium" title="Lithium">lithium</a>, while all heavier elements are synthesised in stars and supernovae. Supernovae tend to enrich the surrounding interstellar medium with elements other than hydrogen and helium, which usually astronomers refer to as "metals".
</p><p>These injected elements ultimately enrich the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular clouds</a> that are the sites of star formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> Thus, each stellar generation has a slightly different composition, going from an almost pure mixture of hydrogen and helium to a more metal-rich composition. Supernovae are the dominant mechanism for distributing these heavier elements, which are formed in a star during its period of nuclear fusion. The different abundances of elements in the material that forms a star have important influences on the star's life, and may decisively influence the possibility of having <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> orbiting it.
</p><p>The kinetic energy of an expanding supernova remnant can trigger star formation by compressing nearby, dense molecular clouds in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> The increase in turbulent pressure can also prevent star formation if the cloud is unable to lose the excess energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aaa128_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-aaa128-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p><p>Evidence from daughter products of short-lived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive_isotope" title="Radioactive isotope">radioactive isotopes</a> shows that a nearby supernova helped determine the composition of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> 4.5 billion years ago, and may even have triggered the formation of this system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 1 June 2020, astronomers reported narrowing down the source of <a href="/wiki/Fast_radio_burst" title="Fast radio burst">Fast Radio Bursts</a> (FRBs), which may now plausibly include "<a href="/wiki/Compact_star" title="Compact star">compact-object</a> mergers and magnetars arising from normal core collapse supernovae".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SA-20200601_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-SA-20200601-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AJL-20200601_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-AJL-20200601-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cosmic_rays">Cosmic rays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Cosmic rays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Supernova remnants are thought to accelerate a large fraction of galactic primary <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>, but direct evidence for cosmic ray production has only been found in a small number of remnants. Gamma rays from <a href="/wiki/Pion" title="Pion">pion</a>-decay have been detected from the supernova remnants <a href="/wiki/IC_443" title="IC 443">IC 443</a> and W44. These are produced when accelerated <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> from the SNR impact on interstellar material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ackermann-2013_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-ackermann-2013-179">[179]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gravitational_waves">Gravitational waves</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Gravitational waves">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Supernovae are potentially strong galactic sources of <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">gravitational waves</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> but none have so far been detected. The only gravitational wave events so far detected are from mergers of black holes and neutron stars, probable remnants of supernovae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morozova_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-morozova-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effect_on_Earth">Effect on Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Effect on Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_supernova" title="Near-Earth supernova">Near-Earth supernova</a></div>
<p>A near-Earth supernova is a supernova close enough to the Earth to have noticeable effects on its <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>. Depending upon the type and energy of the supernova, it could be as far as 3000 light-years away. 
In 1996 it was theorised that traces of past supernovae might be detectable on Earth in the form of metal isotope signatures in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock_strata" title="Rock strata">rock strata</a>. <a href="/wiki/Iron#Isotopes" title="Iron">Iron-60</a> enrichment was later reported in deep-sea rock of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fields_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fields-184">[184]</a></sup> In 2009, elevated levels of nitrate ions were found in Antarctic ice, which coincided with the 1006 and 1054 supernovae. Gamma rays from these supernovae could have boosted atmospheric levels of nitrogen oxides, which became trapped in the ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup>
</p><p>Type Ia supernovae are thought to be potentially the most dangerous if they occur close enough to the Earth. Because these supernovae arise from dim, common white dwarf stars in binary systems, it is likely that a supernova that can affect the Earth will occur unpredictably and in a star system that is not well studied. The closest known candidate is <a href="/wiki/IK_Pegasi" title="IK Pegasi">IK Pegasi</a>, about 150 light-years away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> According to a 2003 estimate, a type II supernova would have to be closer than eight <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">parsecs</a> (26 light-years) to destroy half of the Earth's ozone layer, and there are no such candidates closer than about 500 light-years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gehrels_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gehrels-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Milky_Way_candidates">Milky Way candidates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Supernova&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Milky Way candidates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_supernova_candidates" title="List of supernova candidates">List of supernova candidates</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wolf_rayet2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="641" data-file-width="611" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Wolf_rayet2.jpg/220px-Wolf_rayet2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Wolf_rayet2.jpg/330px-Wolf_rayet2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Wolf_rayet2.jpg/440px-Wolf_rayet2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wolf_rayet2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_nebula" title="Wolf–Rayet nebula">nebula</a> around <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_star" title="Wolf–Rayet star">Wolf–Rayet star</a> WR124, which is located at a distance of about 21,000 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The next supernova in the Milky Way will likely be detectable even if it occurs on the far side of the galaxy. It is likely to be produced by the collapse of an unremarkable red supergiant and it is very probable that it will already have been catalogued in infrared surveys such as <a href="/wiki/2MASS" title="2MASS">2MASS</a>. There is a smaller chance that the next core collapse supernova will be produced by a different type of massive star such as a yellow hypergiant, luminous blue variable, or Wolf–Rayet. The chances of the next supernova being a type Ia produced by a white dwarf are calculated to be about a third of those for a core collapse supernova. Again it should be observable wherever it occurs, but it is less likely that the progenitor will ever have been observed. It isn't even known exactly what a type Ia progenitor system looks like, and it is difficult to detect them beyond a few parsecs. The total supernova rate in our galaxy is estimated to be between 2 and 12 per century, although we haven't actually observed one for several centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adams_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-adams-126">[126]</a></sup>
</p><p>Statistically, the next supernova is likely to be produced from an otherwise unremarkable red supergiant, but it is difficult to identify which of those supergiants are in the final stages of heavy element fusion in their cores and which have millions of years left. The most-massive red supergiants shed their atmospheres and evolve to Wolf–Rayet stars before their cores collapse. All Wolf–Rayet stars end their lives from the Wolf–Rayet phase within a million years or so, but again it is difficult to identify those that are closest to core collapse. One class that is expected to have no more than a few thousand years before exploding are the WO Wolf–Rayet stars, which are known to have exhausted their core helium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> Only eight of them are known, and only four of those are in the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tramper_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-tramper-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>A number of close or well known stars have been identified as possible core collapse supernova candidates: the red supergiants <a href="/wiki/Antares" title="Antares">Antares</a> and <a href="/wiki/Betelgeuse" title="Betelgeuse">Betelgeuse</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> the yellow hypergiant <a href="/wiki/Rho_Cassiopeiae" title="Rho Cassiopeiae">Rho Cassiopeiae</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> the luminous blue variable Eta Carinae that has already produced a supernova impostor;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> and the brightest component, a Wolf–Rayet star, in the Regor or <a href="/wiki/Gamma_Velorum" title="Gamma Velorum">Gamma Velorum</a> system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> Others have gained notoriety as possible, although not very likely, progenitors for a gamma-ray burst; for example <a href="/wiki/WR_104" title="WR 104">WR 104</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>Identification of candidates for a type Ia supernova is much more speculative. Any binary with an accreting white dwarf might produce a supernova although the exact mechanism and timescale is still debated. These systems are faint and difficult to identify, but the novae and recurrent novae are such systems that conveniently advertise themselves. One example is <a href="/wiki/U_Scorpii" title="U Scorpii">U Scorpii</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> The nearest known Type Ia supernova candidate is IK Pegasi (HR 8210), located at a distance of 150 light-years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> but observations suggest it will be several million years before the white dwarf can accrete the critical mass required to become a type Ia supernova.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<li id="cite_note-RTE2021-06-28-01a-123"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-RTE2021-06-28-01a_123-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RTE2021-06-28-01a_123-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-RTE2021-06-28-01a_123-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation news cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.rte.ie/news/newslens/2021/0628/1231824-supernova-crab-nebula/" rel="nofollow">"Astronomers discover new type of supernova"</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RTE_News" title="RTE News">RTE News</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Press_Association" title="Press Association">PA</a>. 28 June 2021. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210630165238/https://www.rte.ie/news/newslens/2021/0628/1231824-supernova-crab-nebula/" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 30 June 2021<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">1 July</span> 2021</span>. <q>In 1980, Ken'ichi Nomoto of the University of Tokyo predicted a third type called an electron capture supernova. ... In an electron capture supernova, as the core runs out of fuel, gravity forces electrons in the core into their atomic nuclei, causing the star to collapse in on itself.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.atitle=Astronomers+discover+new+type+of+supernova&amp;rft.date=2021-06-28&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.rte.ie%2Fnews%2Fnewslens%2F2021%2F0628%2F1231824-supernova-crab-nebula%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASupernova"></span></span>
